  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c85803f7-30d3-4280-a54a-52ebdc6e74e5'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer17437620712707994101">An omnibus edition of three counterculture classics by Richard Brautigan that embody the spirit of the 1960s.<br/> <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is by turns a hilarious, playful, and melancholy novel that wanders from San Francisco through America's rural waterways; <i>In Watermelon Sugar</i> expresses the mood of a new generation, revealing death as a place where people travel the length </span>
  <span id="freeText17437620712707994101" style="display:none">An omnibus edition of three counterculture classics by Richard Brautigan that embody the spirit of the 1960s.<br /> <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is by turns a hilarious, playful, and melancholy novel that wanders from San Francisco through America's rural waterways; <i>In Watermelon Sugar</i> expresses the mood of a new generation, revealing death as a place where people travel the length of their dreams, rejecting violence and hate; and <i>The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster</i> is a collection of nearly 100 poems, first published in 1968.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer34290704" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2485305617202639265">Trout Fishing in America / The Pill vs. the Springhill Mine Disaster / In Watermelon Sugar, Richard Brautigan<br/><br/><b>Trout Fishing in America</b> is a novella written by <b>Richard Brautigan</b> and published in 1967. It is technically Brautigan's first novel; he wrote it in 1961 before A Confederate General From Big Sur, which was published first. <br/><br/>Trout Fishing In America is an abstract book without a clear central story line. Instead, the book contains a series of anecdotes broken into chapters, with the same ch</span>
  <span id="freeText2485305617202639265" style="display:none">Trout Fishing in America / The Pill vs. the Springhill Mine Disaster / In Watermelon Sugar, Richard Brautigan<br /><br /><b>Trout Fishing in America</b> is a novella written by <b>Richard Brautigan</b> and published in 1967. It is technically Brautigan's first novel; he wrote it in 1961 before A Confederate General From Big Sur, which was published first. <br /><br />Trout Fishing In America is an abstract book without a clear central story line. Instead, the book contains a series of anecdotes broken into chapters, with the same characters often reappearing from story to story. <br /><br />The settings of most of the chapters occur in three locales: Brautigan's childhood in the Pacific Northwest of the U.S.; his day-to-day adult life in San Francisco; and a camping trip in Idaho with his wife and infant daughter during the summer of 1961. Most of the chapters were written during this trip. <br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه مارس 1992 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: صید قزل آلا در آمریکا؛ نویسنده: ریچارد براتیگان؛ مترجم پیام یزدانجو؛ تهران، نشر چشمه، 1384؛ در 196ص؛ چاپ دوم 1385؛ شابک 9643622150؛ چاپ سوم 1386؛ شابک 9789643622152؛ چاپ پنجم 1389؛ چاپ ششم 1390؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: صید قزل آلا در آمریکا؛ نویسنده: ریچارد براتیگان؛ مترجم هوشیار انصاری فر؛ تهران، نشر نی، 1385؛ در 207ص؛ شابک 9643138008؛ چاپ دوم 1385؛ چاپ سوم 1388؛<br /><br />رمان «صید قزل آلا در آمریکا‌‏»، نوشته ی «ریچارد براتیگان»، با ترجمه ی جناب «پیام یزدانجو» را نشر چشمه منتشر کرده است؛ «براتیگان»، از نسل نویسندگان دهه ی شصت سده ی بیستم میلادی هستند، و همین کتاب شاهكار ایشانست؛ آثار «براتیگان» بازتاب دهنده ی روح زمانه ی زندگی ایشانست، زمانه ای که درک نو از دنیای تازه ی داستانی را، تا همیشه مرهون نسل «براتیگان»ها خواهد ماند؛ ایشان را نویسنده ای نوگرا، و نامتعارف دانسته اند؛ با همین اثر بود که اشتهار یافتند و نام آور شدند؛ «براتیگان» در این اثر، به بهترین وجه، از نبوغ خویش بهره گرفته، درهای دیگری را به روی ادبیات معاصر، بگشوده است، رمان «صید قزل آلا در آمریكا»، نمونه ی ناب، و یگانه، از ادبیات پسامدرن، و زاییده ی ذهنی خلاق، خارق العاده، و با تخیل تجربی، خیره كننده است؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1357305678" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1357305678" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4015835-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1357305678?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4015835-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1357305678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4990906738191861794">Richard Brautigan is an iconic counter-cultural poet and author who is probably best known for his 1967 novel Trout Fishing in America. His novels deploy a unique blend of magical realism, satire and black comedy. I recently read an omnibus that included two novels Trout Fishing in America and In Watermelon Sugar and a collection of poetry The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster.<br/><br/>I think it is important to understand the life of an author when critically reading their novels. Normally Wikipe</span>
  <span id="freeText4990906738191861794" style="display:none">Richard Brautigan is an iconic counter-cultural poet and author who is probably best known for his 1967 novel Trout Fishing in America. His novels deploy a unique blend of magical realism, satire and black comedy. I recently read an omnibus that included two novels Trout Fishing in America and In Watermelon Sugar and a collection of poetry The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster.<br /><br />I think it is important to understand the life of an author when critically reading their novels. Normally Wikipedia is my starting place and often I find myself going down a rabbit hole of the internet. Richard Brautigan had an interesting life, with interesting ideals. Later in life he was diagnosed with both paranoid schizophrenia and clinical depression, even receiving electroconvulsive therapy as many as twelve times in an effort to treat his condition. Later in his life, he lived life as a recluse and eventually died to a self-inflicted gunshot wound to the head. It is unsure when he died, because his body was found much later.<br /><br />The reason I think author context is important is evident in Brautigan’s book In Watermelon Sugar. The novel tells the story of iDEATH, a futuristic utopian commune that has found a way to live off the land; specifically watermelon sugar. This is told in a first person perspective and Brautigan could be considered the protagonist. However knowing the context of his life, I think Richard Brautigan saw himself more as the antagonist that is ruining this perfect society. Either way, it makes for an interesting read and In Watermelon Sugar was the highlight of this omnibus.<br /><br />Trout Fishing in America was just a weird book, I had gotten use to the style of Richard Brautigan and I knew what to expect. However, Trout Fishing still leaves me perplexed. I know this is a social critique, but I never was able to fully grasp what Brautigan was trying to say. The term Trout Fishing in America became a character’s name, a hotel, a place, and becomes a modifier for just about anything. It was a weird novel and I probably need to do a lot more research to fully understand it.<br /><br />The poetry of Richard Brautigan is just as unique as his novel writing. Most of his poems are short but pack a huge punch. There is so much depth within the poems and sometimes they have the ability to shock. For example, the title poem from this collection The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster, compares birth control to a mining disaster that killed 75 people. He believes that the pill and the disaster both leave life buried forever. Not all his poems pack a punch like this but I thought this poem was a good example of what to expect.<br /><br />I do not think I would have read Richard Brautigan, without the encouragement of Jake from the YouTube channel Tales from iDEATH. This may not be entirely true, since Brautigan is on the 1001 Books list, and still need to read Willard and His Bowling Trophies. I appreciate the push to read Richard Brautigan, he is a weird author, but I enjoyed the experience. His style might irritate many people but I liked the surrealist nature of the three books I read in this omnibus.<br /><br />The review originally appeared on my blog; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.knowledgelost.org/book-reviews/genre/literary-fiction/richard-brautigan/">http://www.knowledgelost.org/book-rev...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4990906738191861794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_207782218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207782218" />
    <a title="Rod" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3973093-rod"><img alt="Rod" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592245072p2/3973093.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207782218?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rod" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rod" href="/user/show/3973093-rod">Rod</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/12602681-trout-fishing-in-america-the-pill-versus-the-springhill-mine-disaster">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=verse">verse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207782218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18045768403408869499"><i>In Watermelon Sugar</i> reminded me, in a certain way, of the Unthank parts of Alasdair Gray's Lanark in that you're never quite sure if the world it inhabits is our world, an alternate dimension, our world in a post-apocalyptic state, or perhaps the afterlife. And I mention "post-apocalyptic" not because it seems like a hostile wasteland (the world it describes is actually quite magical and beautiful), but because there are hints at mysterious "forgotten things," suggesting that perhaps this is a w</span>
  <span id="freeText18045768403408869499" style="display:none"><i>In Watermelon Sugar</i> reminded me, in a certain way, of the Unthank parts of Alasdair Gray's Lanark in that you're never quite sure if the world it inhabits is our world, an alternate dimension, our world in a post-apocalyptic state, or perhaps the afterlife. And I mention "post-apocalyptic" not because it seems like a hostile wasteland (the world it describes is actually quite magical and beautiful), but because there are hints at mysterious "forgotten things," suggesting that perhaps this is a world inhabited by our descendants--but that is only a possibility, and by no means the point. Like Unthank, this world is a dreamlike one that seems to have its own laws of nature and physics, although in watermelon sugar is a pastoral, agrarian existence rather than an urban, industrial one. I very much enjoyed <i>In Watermelon Sugar</i>, but my favorite section of this omnibus was the first and most famous one, <i>Trout Fishing in America</i>. What really knocked me out was that even when it seems outlandish, it is believable, giving the impression that this was something that actually happened to the author or someone he knows, but then was filtered through a twisted lens of poetic vision.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18045768403408869499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24831615-thebookwarren?shelf=fiction-contemporary-drama">fiction-contemporary-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24831615-thebookwarren?shelf=top-shelf-reads">top-shelf-reads</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3367363945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15184898683292588557">What a treat Richard Brautigan is, let alone the uncanny excellence of the treat magnified when you get 3 novella’s in one. <br/><br/>The charm of RB is in what he doesn’t say, what he chooses to leave out.. this forces one to focus on the afterthought, for the prose is so simply efficient it allows your mind to wonder at the wonder!<br/><br/>You don’t read a Brautigan as much as you experience it, live it. Feel it. The book ends up in a newspaper-eque spiral as though it had spent a full day being used at the trac</span>
  <span id="freeText15184898683292588557" style="display:none">What a treat Richard Brautigan is, let alone the uncanny excellence of the treat magnified when you get 3 novella’s in one. <br /><br />The charm of RB is in what he doesn’t say, what he chooses to leave out.. this forces one to focus on the afterthought, for the prose is so simply efficient it allows your mind to wonder at the wonder!<br /><br />You don’t read a Brautigan as much as you experience it, live it. Feel it. The book ends up in a newspaper-eque spiral as though it had spent a full day being used at the track for the racing form, so much do we often slick through back and forth from one poem or story to the next then back again then rinse and repeat over and over! I wonder what Richard Brautigan would think of today? I’d more or less believe he’d be if the Rutger persuasion and feel that humankind is misrepresented en-masse &amp; we all too often are quick to point out the horrific simply due to it being more accessible, more sensationalist .. after all, a report saying ‘no kids kidnapped or wars braking out here’ isn’t exactly one we can ever expect to see in nightly news, our Achilles Heel in modern day, a poison on all of society.<br /><br />I can’t say it better than the Lovely Eggs did..<br /><br />“Have you ever read Richard Brautigan? Ever ever read Brautigan? Well if you’ve never read Richard Brautigan - then I’m fairly sure you’ll burn in hell”..</span>
  <a data-text-id="15184898683292588557" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126396453?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike (the Paladin)" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin">Mike (the Paladin)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126396453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14744413172666109435">I didn't finish this...I only read a small part of it, "sectionally" (different sections in other words). After all, I only have so many hours left in in my life and I choose not to waste too many of them.<br/><br/>Maybe I'm one of those people who doesn't have a regular name, maybe it depends on you? Right. Can you string a group of contradictory words together (I heard through my eyes the feel of orange), can you line up non sequiturs in sentence and paragraph form? Then you to can write poetry as Braut</span>
  <span id="freeText14744413172666109435" style="display:none">I didn't finish this...I only read a small part of it, "sectionally" (different sections in other words). After all, I only have so many hours left in in my life and I choose not to waste too many of them.<br /><br />Maybe I'm one of those people who doesn't have a regular name, maybe it depends on you? Right. Can you string a group of contradictory words together (I heard through my eyes the feel of orange), can you line up non sequiturs in sentence and paragraph form? Then you to can write poetry as Brautigan can.<br /><br />How about putting together a somewhat pretentious pseudo-spiritual novella, possibly based on your experiences in a couple of failed communes? Yeah right.<br /><br />I've read raves about In Watermelon Sugar with claims of spiritual breakthroughs and discovery of transcendental peace. Right. The book puts me in mind of a minor character in The Fountainhead. A writer who writes a book that everyone reads (though the sales figures don't seem to reflect that) and everyone thinks "they understand while no one else does". It gives them a "spiritual feeling" for "some reason". Later even the author gives a wink and a nod over it.<br /><br />If you think you've found the mystery of life through "In Watermelon Sugar" let me suggest you read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15746.One_Door_Away_from_Heaven" title="One Door Away from Heaven by Dean Koontz" rel="nofollow noopener">One Door Away from Heaven</a> by Dean Koontz.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14744413172666109435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381899-dan?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55432846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10779259788994731413">I am mad. Mad that I haven't read any Brautigan until this year. Now that I have this book along with the last he wrote (An Unfortunate Woman) under my reading 'belt' I feel a little happier. This edition, i believe, combines three separate Brautigan works each of which has their merits and their serious entertainment value. There is a wide range of beauty, silliness and emotion in his poetry and the two stories (novels? novellas?) are equally wonderful. Due to his style and layout almost every </span>
  <span id="freeText10779259788994731413" style="display:none">I am mad. Mad that I haven't read any Brautigan until this year. Now that I have this book along with the last he wrote (An Unfortunate Woman) under my reading 'belt' I feel a little happier. This edition, i believe, combines three separate Brautigan works each of which has their merits and their serious entertainment value. There is a wide range of beauty, silliness and emotion in his poetry and the two stories (novels? novellas?) are equally wonderful. Due to his style and layout almost every page of Brautigan's prose seems like it could stand alone without the rest of the story. Luckily for us the rest of the pages exist. <br /><br />Here is the title poem from his book of poetry:<br /><br /><i>The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster<br /><br />When you take your pill<br />it's like the mine disaster.<br />I think if all the people<br /> lost inside of you. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10779259788994731413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3225152" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8603937514208061574">I read these books back in my hippie days and I fell in love with Brautigans prose. It was beautiful, simplistic and minimalist. He sketched pictures that had surprising detail in few words.<br/><br/>BUT...<br/><br/>I've matured. These books will endure as classics in the minds of young liberals with loads of idealism and a longing to be modern-day hobo's, keeping their minds filled with mush and ignoring the complexity of reality.<br/><br/>Still, though, like reading Kerouac's journals, it offers a interesting insight into </span>
  <span id="freeText8603937514208061574" style="display:none">I read these books back in my hippie days and I fell in love with Brautigans prose. It was beautiful, simplistic and minimalist. He sketched pictures that had surprising detail in few words.<br /><br />BUT...<br /><br />I've matured. These books will endure as classics in the minds of young liberals with loads of idealism and a longing to be modern-day hobo's, keeping their minds filled with mush and ignoring the complexity of reality.<br /><br />Still, though, like reading Kerouac's journals, it offers a interesting insight into the thinking of the flower children of the 60's as well as the tragic figure of a man that ultimately couldn't cope. (Brautigan committed suicide right around his 50th birthday). </span>

<div id="review_89507310" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89507310" />
    <a title="Forrest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811928-forrest"><img alt="Forrest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558621304p2/811928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89507310?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forrest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forrest" href="/user/show/811928-forrest">Forrest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89507310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5336968406839852371">You know that stoner from high school, the one with a golden heart and hateful eyes, the one that failed all his classes but had the intelligence to be a Nobel Prize winner, the one that wanted to establish world peace through sarcasm? That's who wrote this book, and it's scary good. Mix equal parts '60s rebelliousness (without overt politics), folksy americana, and surrealism, and you've got it. Snarky anarchy with the best metaphors I've ever read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1696532111" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696532111" />
    <a title="Stacia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia"><img alt="Stacia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316011190p2/3917029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696532111?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stacia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stacia" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia">Stacia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9207144-trout-fishing-in-america-the-pill-versus-the-springhill-mine-disaster">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=north-america">north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=2016-baw-bingo">2016-baw-bingo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696532111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3741816664551591428">I love surreal/experimental writing so this was quite a pleasure for me. It was also a little eye-opening for me to read 'older' surrealism & if I had to sum it up I guess I'd say it's "surreal Americana". I can definitely see that some of the modern surreal/experimental writers I have read have probably taken inspiration from Brautigan.<br/><br/><u>Trout Fishing in America:</u><br/>I saw this described somewhere as poetic prose & I agree. The work is almost like a series of short (under one page up to two pages) ess</span>
  <span id="freeText3741816664551591428" style="display:none">I love surreal/experimental writing so this was quite a pleasure for me. It was also a little eye-opening for me to read 'older' surrealism &amp; if I had to sum it up I guess I'd say it's "surreal Americana". I can definitely see that some of the modern surreal/experimental writers I have read have probably taken inspiration from Brautigan.<br /><br /><u>Trout Fishing in America:</u><br />I saw this described somewhere as poetic prose &amp; I agree. The work is almost like a series of short (under one page up to two pages) essays, all loosely related to trout, fishing, camping, &amp; nature. And all have some kind of surreal bent or observation to them. Trout Fishing in America is often referred to as a character/person, but not always &amp; not in all of the sections. And there's mayonnaise.<br /><br /><u>The Pill <i>versus</i> the Springhill Mine Disaster:</u><br />I'm not a huge fan of poetry, though I liked this collection overall. His poetry is easy to read &amp; most of his poems are very short. Some were amusing, some sad, some touching, some magical, or surreal, or odd.<br /><br /><u>In Watermelon Sugar:</u><br />This is the most story-like piece of the collection &amp; seems like it might be a dystopian, futuristic, or folk/fantasy world -- it's just not quite clear. There are some really beautiful descriptions/ideas in here, including that the town buries people "all in glass coffins at the bottoms of rivers and put foxfire in the tombs, so they glow at night and we can appreciate what comes next." Despite the beauty, this is a melancholy tale set in an offbeat world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3741816664551591428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17003195" />
    <a title="ES" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/963381-es"><img alt="ES" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1205449619p2/963381.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17003195?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ES" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ES" href="/user/show/963381-es">ES</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">burnouts, sesame street characters </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17003195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15994898542478031678">"Dear Trout Fishing in America,"<br/><br/>The silliest thing I've read in awhile, Brautigan uses metaphors that don't really make any sense but feel so original you might tend to take them at face value. Just 'cause it's easier to do so? <br/><br/>"...The other graveyard was for the poor and it had no trees and the grass turned a flat-tire brown in the summer and stayed that way until the rain, like a mechanic, began in the late autumn."<br/><br/>"The FBI agents watched the path, the trees, the black stump, the pool and the </span>
  <span id="freeText15994898542478031678" style="display:none">"Dear Trout Fishing in America,"<br /><br />The silliest thing I've read in awhile, Brautigan uses metaphors that don't really make any sense but feel so original you might tend to take them at face value. Just 'cause it's easier to do so? <br /><br />"...The other graveyard was for the poor and it had no trees and the grass turned a flat-tire brown in the summer and stayed that way until the rain, like a mechanic, began in the late autumn."<br /><br />"The FBI agents watched the path, the trees, the black stump, the pool and the trout as if they were all holes punched in a card that had just come out of a computer. The afternoon sun kept changing everything as it moved across the sky, and the FBI agents kept changing with the sun. It appears to be part of their training."<br /><br />Maybe I'm not making my point about the metaphor's? There's this beauty:<br /><br />"The petals of the vagina unfold<br />like Christopher Columbus<br />taking off his shoes.<br /><br />Is there anything more beautiful<br />than the bow of a ship<br />touching a new world?"<br /><br />Yeah, Brautigan makes me laugh, it's like he's perfected the non-perfection of high-school underground literature. Ironically, he's probably been kicked out of a lot high schools without ever making a physical attempt.<br /><br />I have no idea what the hell I'm going to do with this book, thanks a lot asshole.<br /><br />"Yours Truly,<br /><br />Trout Fishing in America"</span>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer44046507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer623303162232778089">since this volume contained 3 of brautigan's books, let me break down the starred review. the three stars was primarily for 'in watermelon sugar,' which was sad, bizarre and a little haunting. i'd give 1 pithy little star to 'trout fishing in america,' which felt incredibly dusty, dated and drug-addled. 'the pill versus the springhill mine disaster' gets 2 stars for a few beautiful, gut-wrenching poems and a few really really bad ones. i'm glad i read brautigan but at least now i know he is not </span>
  <span id="freeText623303162232778089" style="display:none">since this volume contained 3 of brautigan's books, let me break down the starred review. the three stars was primarily for 'in watermelon sugar,' which was sad, bizarre and a little haunting. i'd give 1 pithy little star to 'trout fishing in america,' which felt incredibly dusty, dated and drug-addled. 'the pill versus the springhill mine disaster' gets 2 stars for a few beautiful, gut-wrenching poems and a few really really bad ones. i'm glad i read brautigan but at least now i know he is not for me.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15285676378483145232">Brautigan's poems are sad and silly and beautiful. His voice is like that of precocious child. Always painfully honest, his work is charming, if not a little kooky.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2865951826" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2865951826" />
    <a title="Brian Bess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1035284-brian-bess"><img alt="Brian Bess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277585693p2/1035284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2865951826?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian Bess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian Bess" href="/user/show/1035284-brian-bess">Brian Bess</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2865951826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17581888538225696912">The lost Beat in his lonely world<br/><br/>Trout Fishing in America<br/>I just read three of Richard Brautigan’s works in an omnibus volume for the first time in forty years. Brautigan is a writer that was unique in that I felt that if you compared him to other notable writers of the 1960’s, he would usually come up short. However, taken for himself and what he was attempting to do, I think he generally succeeded. His work was a blend of metafiction, magical realism, expanded consciousness, and flower power ph</span>
  <span id="freeText17581888538225696912" style="display:none">The lost Beat in his lonely world<br /><br />Trout Fishing in America<br />I just read three of Richard Brautigan’s works in an omnibus volume for the first time in forty years. Brautigan is a writer that was unique in that I felt that if you compared him to other notable writers of the 1960’s, he would usually come up short. However, taken for himself and what he was attempting to do, I think he generally succeeded. His work was a blend of metafiction, magical realism, expanded consciousness, and flower power philosophy that had no equivalent in the works of his contemporaries. He was easy to dismiss and make fun of but he wrote unapologetically in his niche. He was also gentle and idealistic, with no trace that I could detect of the irony or cynicism that is so prevalent in the work of the 21st century.<br /><br />‘Trout Fishing in America’ is the book that he’s probably best known for writing. It was his first “novel”, although nothing about it is novelistic in the way of plot or character evolution. The book is perhaps more of a collection of absurdist, surreal mini-essays on themes that generally tie in to this idea of Trout Fishing in America, which he has gone so far as to personify. Trout Fishing in America is a mythic entity in Brautigan’s world.<br /><br />It is also, like many of his other fictional works, a first-person narrative. The “I” character can easily be interpreted to be some version of Brautigan himself. This is reinforced by the cover photograph, which shows Brautigan with his lady friend posed in front of a well-known statue of Ben Franklin in San Francisco. The cover photo is referred to multiple times throughout the book so he wants us to consider the cover as just as essential to the book to the contents within.<br /><br />One tactic that Brautigan takes to a greater extent than any other writer I’ve read is that he will take a metaphor and allow the metaphor to overtake the reality. For example, in the chapter titled “The Hunchback Trout.” He begins with the statement, “The creek was like 12,845 telephone booths in a row with high Victorian ceilings and all the doors taken off and all the backs of the booths blocked out.” This prompts the next sentence: Sometimes when I went fishing in there, I felt just like a telephone repairman, even though I did not look like one. I was only a kid covered with fishing tackle, but in some strange way by going in there and catching a few trout, I kept the telephones in service.” From then on in the chapter he refers to the creek as telephone booths and himself as a telephone repairman. He allows that image to dominate how he approaches the simple activity of catching a hunchback trout.<br /><br />The image of trout fishing evokes Ernest Hemingway’s various stories about hunting and fishing and the fact isn’t lost on Brautigan. He recalls finding out about Hemingway’s suicide by shotgun (eerily prophetic as that is the method Brautigan himself used to end his life over 20 years later). Brautigan doesn’t just refer to the idea of trout fishing or write about fishing experiences, he personifies it. Trout Fishing in America is an entity that lives in the forms of a sixth grade “terrorist” group that writes on the backs of the jackets of first graders the phrase, “Trout Fishing in America”. Trout Fishing in America encounters FBI agents searching for a ‘ten-most wanted” criminal who claims to love trout fishing. Finally, the narrator has a conversation with Trout Fishing in America when he is fishing in the Big Wood River, a few miles from Ketcham, Idaho, where Hemingway killed himself. They discuss memorable fishing sites and various memorable fish that were never caught.<br /><br />In an ultimate surrealistic anecdote that also comments on the popularity of outdoor sports, the narrator tells about a place called the Cleveland Wrecking Yard that sells used trout streams:<br />‘”Can I help you?”<br />“Yes, I said. “I’m curious about the trout streams you have for sale. Can you tell me something about it? How are you selling it?”<br />“We’re selling it by the foot length. You can buy as little as you want or you can buy all we’ve got left. A man came in here this morning and bought 563 feet. He’s going to give it to his niece for a birthday present,” the salesman said.<br />“We’re selling the waterfalls separately of course, and the trees and birds, flowers, grass and ferns we’re also selling extra. The insects we’re giving away free with a minimum purchase of ten feet of stream.”<br />“How much are you selling the stream for?” I asked.<br />“Six dollars and fifty-cents a foot,” he said. “That’s for the first hundred feet. After that it’s five dollars a foot.”<br /><br />The exchange continues along these lines and finally the narrator asks where the stream came from. “Colorado”, the salesman replies. “We moved it with loving care. We’ve never damaged a trout stream yet. We treat them as if they were china.”<br /><br />The narrator explores the store and indeed finds trout streams, waterfalls, various animals, insects, birds, and even wanders into the used plumbing department where he finds stacks of toilets piled on top of one another.<br /><br />The book ends generally where it begins and one leaves the book realizing that it could begin and end anywhere, meandering its own way, much like a trout stream.<br /><br /><br />The Pill versus the Springhill Mining Disaster<br />This collection of poems is defined by the same guiding principle that led to the writing of ‘Trout Fishing in America’. Few of the poems are longer than a page. Many take up less than half of a page. Some are short as haikus. In fact, some could be considered haikus themselves. It’s easier to understand the effect of these poems by sampling a few of them.<br /><br />Karma Repair Kit Items 1-4<br />1.	Get enough food to eat,<br />and eat it<br />2.	Find a place to sleep where it is quiet,<br />and sleep there.<br />3.	Reduce intellectual and emotional noise<br />Until you arrive at the silence of yourself,<br />and listen to it.<br />4.	 <br /><br />Love Poem<br /> It’s so nice<br />to wake up in the morning<br /> all alone<br />and not have to tell somebody you love them<br />when you don’t love them<br /> any more. <br /><br />And the title poem:<br /><br />When you take your pill<br />it’s like a mine disaster.<br />I think of all the people<br />lost inside of you. <br /><br />Multiply one of these poems by about 100, and you have a good indication of the experience of reading ‘The Pill versus the Springhill Mine Disaster’.<br /><br /><br />In Watermelon Sugar<br />This novella actually has a coherent narrative structure. The opening circular sentence sets the tone for what follows: “In watermelon sugar the deeds were done and done again as my life is done in watermelon sugar.”<br /><br />The narrator (Brautigan? He’s on the cover again) lives in this unusual settlement at an indeterminate time in a settlement which has an Edenic serenity to it. He lives in a shack on the outskirts and there is something resembling a downtown area but the center of the settlement, the communal gathering place where people eat and socialize, is a place called iDEATH. That name immediately evokes the death of the i, being the ego, where the occupants live in peace and harmony. They process watermelons, cook them down to their essence—‘watermelon sugar’—and somehow incorporate said sugar into building planks so in essence, their houses and buildings are made from watermelon sugar. The narrator remembers being a child in the area with his parents, when tigers came and ate them. These were talking tigers. They didn’t hurt him, just asked what he was doing. He said he was working on arithmetic homework so they helped him. He wrote one of the first books written in the area for the last forty years. It was about weather. He’s hard at work on a new book, not about weather, but won’t tell any of his fellow citizens exactly what it’s about yet.<br /><br />The tigers eventually lost the power of speech and moved on. The sun changes color each day and is somehow connected to the watermelons. Monday is red, with red watermelons, Tuesday is golden, sun and watermelons, Wednesday, gray, Thursday, black, soundless watermelons (there is no sound, including human speech on these days), Friday, white, Saturday, blue, and Sunday, brown with brown watermelons. Each day then has its own color.<br /><br />The narrator is “going steady” with Pauline, who does most of the cooking at iDEATH. Pauline was best friends with Margaret, the narrator’s former girlfriend. For unspecified reasons, he and Pauline fell in love and Margaret was heartbroken. She stills comes to his shack but he ignores her knock. Beyond the boundaries of the town lie the Forgotten Works. This is a decrepit wilderness, essentially a giant junkyard of discarded, useless items. The main occupants there are “inBOIL and that gang of his.” inBOIL is the brother of one of the town elders who grew more dissipated and sodden by alcohol. He and his gang are unwashed, crude, and rude. inBOIL looks threatening although he mainly comes around to say things like “You don’t know what iDEATH is. I’ll show you what iDEATH really is.” The threat is realized one day when they come to the town, demonstrating iDEATH by mutilating themselves, cutting off thumbs, noses, other body parts, and bleeding to death. Margaret, already depressed, has become fascinated with inBOIL and that gang of his and has explored the Forgotten Works. After their “iDEATH”, she hangs herself.<br /><br />The entire story is told in this serene, deadpan tone. Brautigan still can’t get away from trouts, though. He beings in “the Grand Old Trout’, an old trout raised in the trout hatchery at iDEATH. He has grown big and old and comes downstream out of curiosity. He and the narrator just stare at each other. They recognize each other from a time when they were both much younger.<br /><br />In this story, Brautigan has actually harnessed his metaphors to a coherent, though strange, narrative. Is iDEATH really a new Eden? Is inBOIL a new Cain, who directs his rage inwardly rather than killing his brother? I think there’s sufficient room for such an interpretation given the choice of words. For me, it is a memorable psychedelic fantasy that I first read in my early twenties that has left an impression and stayed with me to this day. I doubt if Brautigan will ever come back in fashion to the extent that he ever was in the 1960’s. However, I think he’s worth checking out, not just as a relic from an earlier time but as someone who, for better and worse, let his imagination run wild and free, something we could all benefit from today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17581888538225696912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_484689208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484689208" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7559863-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329285270p2/7559863.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484689208?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/7559863-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7559863-matt?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484689208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer792058148587965753">The collection opens with Trout Fishing in America, which is a series really short stories or prose poems (depending who you ask, and most would agree that either definition fits) that hang together cohesively but loosely. The method of metaphor and transformation of America from one era into another is as masterful as it is entertaining. The matters of words and history also play a strong role, as various literary critics have observed. Even if most of the symbolism doesn't stick for you, the p</span>
  <span id="freeText792058148587965753" style="display:none">The collection opens with Trout Fishing in America, which is a series really short stories or prose poems (depending who you ask, and most would agree that either definition fits) that hang together cohesively but loosely. The method of metaphor and transformation of America from one era into another is as masterful as it is entertaining. The matters of words and history also play a strong role, as various literary critics have observed. Even if most of the symbolism doesn't stick for you, the pervasive feeling of something marvelous changing into something we don't yet know would be hard to miss; there is America that was, is, and will be, reflected in the stories' narrator's life as it transitions similarly.<br /><br />The Pill versus the Springhill Mine Disaster is often interpreted oddly, I think. Many people encountering this kind of Beat/post-Beat poetry for the first time tend to not fair well with it; Brautigan is not the most approachable of styles to start out with. While there are some gems, and I've come to enjoy this collection more each time I read it, overall it doesn't rank among my favorites from the era.<br /><br />In Watermelon Sugar is a very emotional bit of writing. It has many instances of over-the-top symbolism and operates on that premise without always being so obvious with what it's trying to say: that is a tricky balance. Where Trout Fishing in America relies on a more complex writing style, the narrative voice of In Watermelon Sugar is straightforward to the point of often seeming childish in its guilelessness. It opens the doors for interpretative analysis even more than Trout Fishing in America does, but as a result it seems to have less significance. It feels a bit displaced. That being said, I really enjoy it and find it fun to talk about. It manages to draw on pretty compelling moods and emotions, having disguised its depth in its direct and simple narrative style.<br /><br />All in all, Trout Fishing in America outshines the other two works tremendously, but In Watermelon Sugar is still definitely worth the read, and The Pill versus the Springhill Mine Disaster goes by quickly, you'll probably find something to like about it for its strange diversity, and when you reread it more connections will arise making it more enjoyable than the first time. People who don't like this work either don't like that "getting it" can be a somewhat hazy and unrewarding accomplishment; whereas reading and enjoying the creative style, as well as some of the implications it provides to be drawn upon, is really what I think the experience of these works of Brautigan are about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="792058148587965753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101221651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101221651" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/588949-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194926937p2/588949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101221651?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/588949-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101221651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1759167081966365622">It's rare that you find a book as experimental yet playful as "Trout Fishing in America." Normally I don't have the patience for novels that emphasize clever structure over compelling storyline. But the short stories in "Trout Fishing" are breezy reads that keep you reading because of Brautigan's lyrical, poetic writing. The characters themselves aren't as memorable as the portraits he paints of escapes into nature and the strange ways he twists the words "trout fishing in America" into differen</span>
  <span id="freeText1759167081966365622" style="display:none">It's rare that you find a book as experimental yet playful as "Trout Fishing in America." Normally I don't have the patience for novels that emphasize clever structure over compelling storyline. But the short stories in "Trout Fishing" are breezy reads that keep you reading because of Brautigan's lyrical, poetic writing. The characters themselves aren't as memorable as the portraits he paints of escapes into nature and the strange ways he twists the words "trout fishing in America" into different roles in his stories.<br /><br />My favorite part of the book, though, is the poetry collected in "The Pill Versus the Springhill Mine Disaster." The in-love/out-of-love poems are especially tender (my favorite is "I Feel Horrible. She Doesn't.") Again, his playfulness with words pulls you into a happy hippie headspace that's fun to revel in.<br /><br />"In Watermelon Sugar" had a lot of cool imagery involving watermelons, but it was the least interesting of the three-part book. It moves quickly through a strange tale of a divided community and a man divided between lovers, and while it's original in the formatting, it's the least poetic of the works collected here. At least I now know where the band inBOIL got its name. Ha. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1759167081966365622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1006327242" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_2760260852" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2760260852" />
    <a title="Linda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84132302-linda"><img alt="Linda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543469346p2/84132302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2760260852?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda" href="/user/show/84132302-linda">Linda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84132302-linda?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84132302-linda?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84132302-linda?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/84132302-linda?shelf=unique-genre">unique-genre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2760260852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2502785495128705914"> Always spoiler free. This will be the most difficult review I have ever done because this book is beyond description. Richard Brautigan was born in 1935 and met a tragic death at the age of 49. He was a counter culture phenomenon and literary idol in the 1960s and early 1970s, especially among the young followers of the cultural upheavals and ideals of that time. His works have been called comic genius and an iconoclastic vision of American life.<br/> He has a unique and humorous way of using metaph</span>
  <span id="freeText2502785495128705914" style="display:none"> Always spoiler free. This will be the most difficult review I have ever done because this book is beyond description. Richard Brautigan was born in 1935 and met a tragic death at the age of 49. He was a counter culture phenomenon and literary idol in the 1960s and early 1970s, especially among the young followers of the cultural upheavals and ideals of that time. His works have been called comic genius and an iconoclastic vision of American life.<br /> He has a unique and humorous way of using metaphors that can at times be laugh out loud funny and at times be "What?" followed by a puzzled giggle. Things that occur in his books and poems are often nonsensical or perplexing and sometimes left me reading on just to find out what weird thing would happen next. He presents his materials in a rather matter-of-fact manner that some people would probably find really boring. The humor and off the wall ideas are what kept me reading (except for one of the three "books" which I will tell you about). <br /> The first book is "Trout Fishing in America" which was a hit selling over 2 million copies over the world. It's not a novel. It's more a collection of "slice of life" items related to the title written in the first person. Each chapter is 2-4 pages long and the total, in paperback 112 pages. I tried to read this on two different occasions and failed miserably. Maybe one needs to be smoking dope when they read it? lol<br /> The second book, "The Pill versus the Springhill Mine Disaster" is actually a collection of short poems. They are mostly quite fun to read due to the metaphors and odd viewpoints. Many of the poems are 5 lines long and none are more than slightly over 1 page. The poems are all free verse with no rhyme or rhythm.<br /> The last book is In Watermelon Sugar. It took me 2 tries to get through this one. I'm glad I tried a second time. It was truly peculiar and kept me a little off kilter much of the time but in a fun way. I just never knew what would come next. I loved the odd humor and metaphors. It is a very "matter of fact" telling of a tale of life in a small community under peculiar circumstances. It could also be called "slice of life" but a very baffling life. The ending was hinted at early in the book but was far from expected and I don't think I'll ever be the same after reading it (but in a stunned way).<br /> I think the readership for a book like this may be somewhat limited. If you read "The Hawkline Monster" you got a feel for the metaphors which is why I chose to read this book. But this book is much more bizarre than Hawkline Monster. Reading this book is a chance to read a genre that doesn't exist, as far as I can tell. It's a chance to read a product reflecting a peculiarity produced by the Hippy culture. <br /> I'm glad I read this (or most of it) because it was an experience I don't expect to ever find anywhere else. I also enjoyed the last 2 sections. But be forewarned....some people think I'm weird. lol</span>
  <a data-text-id="2502785495128705914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3137194231" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3137194231" />
    <a title="Herman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40706083-herman"><img alt="Herman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596816409p2/40706083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3137194231?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Herman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Herman" href="/user/show/40706083-herman">Herman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3137194231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2747252470160228767">Synopsis: It sucked! Move on. <br/><br/>Explanation when I was a teenager my father’s store was next door to a tiny, cramped little bookshop that boasted selling second-, third- , and fourthhand books of all genres, here was where I found fat paperbacks with curling covers, here were my favorites from the seventies, books that made me the romantic book lover that I am today, Go ask Alice, Hunter S Thompson, John Steinbeck, William Burroughs, Kurt Vonnegut, and yes Richard Brautigan, books on shelves, on s</span>
  <span id="freeText2747252470160228767" style="display:none">Synopsis: It sucked! Move on. <br /><br />Explanation when I was a teenager my father’s store was next door to a tiny, cramped little bookshop that boasted selling second-, third- , and fourthhand books of all genres, here was where I found fat paperbacks with curling covers, here were my favorites from the seventies, books that made me the romantic book lover that I am today, Go ask Alice, Hunter S Thompson, John Steinbeck, William Burroughs, Kurt Vonnegut, and yes Richard Brautigan, books on shelves, on stools, or in stacks on the floor, piled to the ceiling. Shopping here always brought to mind the scene from Fellowship of the Ring, Gandalf digging through scrolls at Minis Tirith.<br />I had warm feelings toward Brautigan and his story the Hawkline Monster, but times presses us forward and it’s been awhile since I’ve read a Brautigan novel last year I picked up “Dreaming of Babylon” and I wrote of that book “A comedy that is not very funny it feels forced and lacking in substance a plot that runs on zaniness relying too heavily on some tired joke about dreaming of Babylon, that wasn’t all that funny the first dozen times I read it. Very disappointed”. This Christmas we were traveling and as I love to do I visit bookstores along the way found a nice one with a wide selection of the types of books I enjoy reading and there was these three books in one collection of Brautigan stories even though the last book I read of his was terrible I decided it give it one last chance see if I could recapture the magic, No ot even close. This year I made a decision not to give up on a book even if I don’t like it no more adding to my ‘Can not finish list’ just gut it out and move on. Well it’s taken longer than I would have liked and I regret buying this book Brautigan is dead to me now, especially after this book which was awful maybe one or two poems were not that bad but the rest of this was the ravings of a shallow not so talented writer who took too much LSD and thought the insipid musings that flowed out of his mangled conscience was of interest to anyone it not at least not to me. One Star and a great waste of my reading time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2747252470160228767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155050377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155050377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6422579180079320238">This is one of those books whose appeal I totally understand, but also don't really love. Which is to say: a lot of my talented, smart friends view this as a seminal book, and would cite Brautigan as one of their most influential writers, but maybe I came to it at the wrong time? There are moments of absolute, objective brilliance, when he's funny and sad and weird and accessible all at the same time, and then there are moments where my patience for seemingly arbitrary and folksy weirdness runs </span>
  <span id="freeText6422579180079320238" style="display:none">This is one of those books whose appeal I totally understand, but also don't really love. Which is to say: a lot of my talented, smart friends view this as a seminal book, and would cite Brautigan as one of their most influential writers, but maybe I came to it at the wrong time? There are moments of absolute, objective brilliance, when he's funny and sad and weird and accessible all at the same time, and then there are moments where my patience for seemingly arbitrary and folksy weirdness runs thin. I love that Brautigan's books exist and I love that they've led to some other books I really like, but I didn't love actually reading these. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_126285437" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126285437" />
    <a title="Dayna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2803167-dayna"><img alt="Dayna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335928747p2/2803167.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126285437?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dayna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dayna" href="/user/show/2803167-dayna">Dayna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126285437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7500403164083349920">Fantastic. Somewhat eye-crossing, in that this particular volume combines a few of Brautigan's books-- including a volume of largely excellent and surprising poetry-- and thus I must return to it (not an unpleasant task!) to take my time over each work in turn. Trout Fishing in America, while by far the least accessible work in the compilation in terms of prose, got me sort of humming in its groove. You could hear it stream by in your head, a voice from that particular America, the dry, lightly </span>
  <span id="freeText7500403164083349920" style="display:none">Fantastic. Somewhat eye-crossing, in that this particular volume combines a few of Brautigan's books-- including a volume of largely excellent and surprising poetry-- and thus I must return to it (not an unpleasant task!) to take my time over each work in turn. Trout Fishing in America, while by far the least accessible work in the compilation in terms of prose, got me sort of humming in its groove. You could hear it stream by in your head, a voice from that particular America, the dry, lightly skipping voice of that ramblin' man reeling out in a line of cigarette (bummed) smoke tickled by the passing mountain breeze. Good stuff. Frequently funny in such a way I laughed out loud. The perfect primer to enter into The Pill versus the Springhill Mine Disaster (the poetry volume), so that each perceptive slice-- often striking and moving, more often delightful and wicked-- pops like a spoken parcel into your mind's ear, a quick flash of pinwheel behind your eyes. In Watermelon Sugar will be the particular recipient of my second lingering look, as it begs seventy or so in sequence-- I may be able to re-read it for the rest of my life, as the mood strikes. And it shall, when I least expect it. A strange, delicately brushed portrait of an equally strange place of half-life-- supersaturated with meditation and confusion-- in the constant presence of death and knowing only symbolism. Giant statues of vegetables. Peaceful, oddly removed citizens concerned with rivers, soft lights, glass coffins. . . Renegade ruffians living at the edge of a vast junkyard of all our yesterdays. . . people who ask mild questions and receive sudden, violent answers. Very quiet. Very fine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7500403164083349920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4139529" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4139529" />
    <a title="Talia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/242201-talia"><img alt="Talia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1196145828p2/242201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4139529?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Talia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Talia" href="/user/show/242201-talia">Talia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/242201-talia?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/242201-talia?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4139529" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2513102111947302572">I had to lower my rating. I had read this book several years ago, as a teenager in love with the idea of being a rebel and to me, Brautigan was. So, perhaps when I rated it I added nostalgia: remembering the purple capret and insence burning while someone read to me, etc. But it's hard to judge something written in a differnt decade with differnt stuff floating around in the air and different sort of literary things going on. I wasn't there. I can't say much about in context of its own. But ther</span>
  <span id="freeText2513102111947302572" style="display:none">I had to lower my rating. I had read this book several years ago, as a teenager in love with the idea of being a rebel and to me, Brautigan was. So, perhaps when I rated it I added nostalgia: remembering the purple capret and insence burning while someone read to me, etc. But it's hard to judge something written in a differnt decade with differnt stuff floating around in the air and different sort of literary things going on. I wasn't there. I can't say much about in context of its own. But there is still so much original stuff to admire and I would be a fool to overlook it. For example:<br /><br />I Feel Horrible. She Doesn't<br /><br />I feel horrible. She doesn't<br />love me and I wander around<br />the house like a sewing machine<br />that's just finished sewing<br />a turd to a garbage can lid.<br /><br /><br />and<br /><br />Boo, Forever<br /><br />Spinning like a ghost <br />on the bottom of a<br /> top,<br />I'm haunted by all<br />the space that I<br />will live without<br /> you.<br /><br />I can see how I went crazy over a poem titled "The Day They Busted the Grateful Dead" back then. <br /><br />I love "In Watermelon Sugar" for its absurdity.<br /> <br />"of course we used a lot less tombs when the tigers were in bloom. But now we bury them all in glass coffins at the bottoms of rivers. And we put foxfires in the tombs so they glow at night and we can appreciate what comes next."<br /><br />And the tigers that ate the father; a classic poor adolescent from the get-go, perhaps like Harry Potter himself. But all of that doesn't compensate for the repetition of Brautigan's declarative sentence. Quite monotonous when read page after page. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2513102111947302572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_49510514" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49510514" />
    <a title="Ruth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/316631-ruth"><img alt="Ruth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1204062236p2/316631.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49510514?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruth" href="/user/show/316631-ruth">Ruth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49510514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9486457584590603836">I found this one randomly at the library, and I opened it to a utopian poem about a forest with deer and animals who are coexisting peacefully with computers- "watched over by machines of loving grace"- and I had to read more. The author is this crazy San Francisco hippie and Trout Fishing in America is loose sort of novel about wandering, while The Pill vs... is a collection of poems. My favorite part, though, was In Watermelon Sugar, also a novel. It seemed to take place in this post-apocalypt</span>
  <span id="freeText9486457584590603836" style="display:none">I found this one randomly at the library, and I opened it to a utopian poem about a forest with deer and animals who are coexisting peacefully with computers- "watched over by machines of loving grace"- and I had to read more. The author is this crazy San Francisco hippie and Trout Fishing in America is loose sort of novel about wandering, while The Pill vs... is a collection of poems. My favorite part, though, was In Watermelon Sugar, also a novel. It seemed to take place in this post-apocalyptic world where all of the things of the old world were piled up in this junkyard and nobody knew what to do with them. And there were some amazingly weird things about this world, like the sun shone a different color every day, and everything in the town (like building materials) was made of "watermelon sugar". And depending on the day you planted the watermelon, whatever color the sun was shining would be the color the watermelon would come out. One of the colors (and watermelon varieties) was black silence, so it wasn't just a color, it was also a quality of things not making noises. And they ate communally and they buried their dead in glowing glass coffins underwater. And there were statues of vegetables all around the town created by somebody who really liked vegetables. It sounds crazy but I kind of wanted to live in this town. Except I wouldn't have wanted to be there during the tiger attacks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9486457584590603836" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
